[2;1m>[22;22m 
Reasoning:
- The user wants to know what happens to the resources deployed by an Argo CD Application when the Application resource itself is deleted via `kubectl delete`.

The Argo CD Application resource defines how resources are deployed.
When an Application is deleted from the Argo CD cluster, Argo CD stops managing it.
Crucially, deleting the Application CRD object itself does *not* trigger a cleanup of the deployed resources, unless specific mechanisms are in place (like Helm hooks or specific Argo CD settings, but by default, it doesn't).
Actually, Argo CD's default behavior is that deleting the Application object leaves the deployed resources in place. This is a common gotcha. To automatically prune resources when an Application is deleted, you typically need to rely on the `syncPolicy.automated.prune` but that only applies when the Application is synced/updated, not when the Application object itself is deleted. Wait, actually, there's a feature called `finalizers` or specific behavior? No, standard Argo CD behavior: deleting the Application CR does not delete the deployed resources. They are orphaned in the cluster.

Let's verify this.
From Argo CD documentation: "Deleting an Application will not delete the resources it deployed. To automatically delete resources when the Application is deleted, you can use the `--prune` flag with `argocd app delete` or configure finalizers, but `kubectl delete application/jgw-app` simply removes the Application resource. The deployed resources remain."
Actually, `argocd app delete --force` might prune, but `kubectl delete` just removes the CR.
Therefore, the Application is deleted, but the child resources are NOT deleted.

This matches option B.

Let's double check.
Argo CD Application lifecycle:
- Create/Update Application -> ArgO CD controller syncs resources.
- Delete Application -> ArgO CD controller removes the Application from its cache, but does not touch the cluster resources. The resources remain.
This is a well-known Argo CD behavior. Option B is correct.

Confirming the exact wording of the options:
A) The Application and all child resources of the Application are deleted.
B) The Application is deleted, but the child resources of the Application are not deleted.
C) The Application is deleted, but only the immediate child resources of the Application are deleted.
D) The Application is not deleted, because the child resources are not deleted.

Answer is B.
I will output only "B".
Check for any recent changes in Argo CD? As of v2.x/v3.x, this behavior remains standard. Deleting the Application object itself does not trigger pruning. You have to use `argocd app delete --prune` or rely on garbage collection if set, but standard `kubectl delete` just removes the CR.

Thus, B is correct.
Final answer: B. 
No extra text.

Reasoning:
- The user is asking about the behavior of Argo CD when an Application resource is deleted using `kubectl delete`.

The Argo CD Application definition provided includes:
```yaml
  finalizers:
    - resources-finalizer.argocd.argoproj.io
```

This finalizer `resources-finalizer.argoproj.io` instructs Argo CD to delete the resources managed by the Application when the Application object itself is deleted. Without this finalizer, `kubectl delete application/jgw-app` would only delete the Application object, leaving the child resources intact (orphaned). With this finalizer, Argo CD's controller will first delete the child resources in the cluster, and then allow the Application object to be deleted.

So, the correct behavior is that both the Application and all its child resources are deleted.

Looking at the options:
A) The Application and all child resources of the Application are deleted.
B) The Application is deleted, but the child resources of the Application are not deleted.
C) The Application is deleted, but only the immediate child resources of the Application are deleted.
D) The Application is not deleted, because the child resources are not deleted.

Option A matches the behavior dictated by the `resources-finalizer.argocd.argoproj.io` finalizer.

Let's double check.
Argo CD documentation says: "When an Application is deleted, the system will also delete the resources managed by the Application. This behavior can be disabled by removing the `resources-finalizer.argocd.argoproj.io` finalizer from the Application manifest."
Since the finalizer is present, the child resources will be deleted.
Therefore, A is correct.

[2;1m>[22;22m 
[2;1m>[22;22m What happens to the resources that were deployed via the Argo CD Application:
[2;1m>[22;22m A) The Application and all child resources of the Application are deleted.
[2;1m>[22;22m B) The Application is deleted, but the child resources of the Application are not deleted.
[2;1m>[22;22m C) The Application is deleted, but only the immediate child resources of the Application are deleted.
[2;1m>[22;22m D) The Application is not deleted, because the child resources are not deleted.
[2;1m>[22;22m 
[2;1m>[22;22m Provide ONLY the answer. The answer will be a single letter from the multiple-choice list. Don't write any other text.
[2;1m>[22;22m 
Reasoning:
- The user wants to know the outcome of running `argocd app delete jgw-app --cascade=false` on an Argo CD Application.

1.  **Analyze the command**: `argocd app delete <app-name>` deletes the Application object from Argo CD.
2.  **Analyze the flag**: `--cascade=false` (or `--cascade` without a value usually defaults to true in some contexts, but here it's explicitly set to false).
3.  **Understanding Argo CD Deletion**:
    *   When an Argo CD Application is deleted, Argo CD checks if there are resources in the destination cluster that belong to that application.
    *   If `--cascade=false` is passed, it tells Argo CD *not* to orchestrate the deletion of the resources deployed by the application. It only removes the Application CRD object itself.
    *   If `--cascade=true` (default behavior in many cases if not specified, or explicitly set), Argo CD will send delete requests for the manifests it manages.
4.  **Evaluate the options**:
    *   A) *The Application and all child resources of the Application are deleted.* -> This happens if cascade is true (or default).
    *   B) *The Application is deleted, but the child resources of the Application are not deleted.* -> This matches `--cascade=false`. The Application object is removed, but the resources (Deployments, Services, etc.) created by it remain in the cluster.
    *   C) *The Application is deleted, but only the immediate child resources of the Application are deleted.* -> Argo CD doesn't typically distinguish between immediate/non-immediate children for deletion in this context; it deletes manifests it tracks. Regardless, `--cascade=false` stops deletion.
    *   D) *The Application is not deleted, because the child resources are not deleted.* -> This is incorrect. The flag controls whether child resources are deleted, it doesn't block the deletion of the Application itself.

5.  **Conclusion**: The flag `--cascade=false` explicitly instructs Argo CD to delete the Application but leave the resources (children) alone.

6.  **Final Answer Selection**: Option B.

Let's double check the Argo CD documentation/command reference.
`argocd app delete`
`--cascade`: Cascade application delete to all its resources (default true).
If `--cascade=false` is provided, the Application is deleted, but the resources are not.

Therefore, B is the correct answer.

```
Then deletes it:
```

```

If this finalizer is removed, Argo CD will not delete the child resources when the Application object is deleted. The Application object itself will be deleted, but the resources it deployed will remain in the cluster.

Let's verify this behavior.

[2;1m>[22;22m kubectl delete app jgw-app -n jgw
[2;1m>[22;22m ```
[2;1m>[22;22m 
[2;1m>[22;22m What happens to the resources that were deployed via the Argo CD Application:
[2;1m>[22;22m A) The Application and all child resources of the Application are deleted.
[2;1m>[22;22m B) The Application is deleted, but the child resources of the Application are not deleted.
[2;1m>[22;22m C) The Application is deleted, but only the immediate child resources of the Application are deleted.
[2;1m>[22;22m D) The Application is not deleted, because the child resources are not deleted.
[2;1m>[22;22m 
[2;1m>[22;22m Provide ONLY the answer. The answer will be a single letter from the multiple-choice list. Don't write any other text.
[2;1m>[22;22m 
Reasoning:
- Here's a thinking process:

1.  **Analyze User Input:**
   - **Argo CD Application YAML:** Standard setup with `syncPolicy.automated.prune: true` and `selfHeal: true`. It's synced and healthy.
   - **Commands executed:**
     ```
     kubectl patch app jgw-app -n jgw -p '{"metadata": {"finalizers": ["resources-finalizer.argocd.argoproj.io"]}}' --type merge
     kubectl delete app jgw-app -n jgw
     ```
   - **Question:** What happens to the resources deployed via the Argo CD Application?
   - **Options:**
     A) The Application and all child resources of the Application are deleted.
     B) The Application is deleted, but the child resources of the Application are not deleted.
     C) The Application is deleted, but only the immediate child resources of the Application are deleted.
     D) The Application is not deleted, because the child resources are not deleted.

2.  **Identify Key Concepts:**
   - Argo CD Application finalizers: `resources-finalizer.argocd.argoproj.io` vs `resources-finalizer.argoproj.io` (note the slight variation in naming, but Argo CD specifically uses `resources-finalizer.argocd.argoproj.io` or `resources-finalizer.argoproj.io` depending on version/context).
   - Actually, Argo CD documentation states: By default, when an Application is deleted, the resources created by Argo CD are also deleted (because of `prune: true`). However, you can prevent deletion of the live cluster resources by adding a specific finalizer to the Application's metadata: `resources-finalizer.argocd.argoproj.io`.
   - When this finalizer is present, deleting the Application removes the Application CRD object, but *keeps* the deployed resources on the cluster. This is often used for "orphaning" resources or when moving to a new CI/CD system.
   - Let's verify the exact finalizer name: Argo CD docs say `resources-finalizer.argocd.argoproj.io`. (Sometimes just `resources-finalizer` in older docs, but the prompt uses the full one).
   - The command patches the Application to add this finalizer, then deletes it.
   - With this finalizer, Argo CD will delete the Application object but *not* prune/delete the deployed resources.

3.  **Evaluate Options:**
   - A) Application and all child resources deleted. (Incorrect, this happens without the finalizer and with prune enabled)
   - B) Application is deleted, but child resources are not deleted. (Matches the behavior of the finalizer)
   - C) Application deleted, only immediate children deleted. (Incorrect)
   - D) Application not deleted. (Incorrect, kubectl delete succeeds)

4.  **Confirm with Documentation/Knowledge:**
   - Argo CD Application Finalizers: `resources-finalizer.argocd.argoproj.io` prevents automatic cleanup of managed resources when the Application is deleted.
   - Reference: https://argo-cd.readthedocs.io/en/stable/user-guide/resource_finalizers/
   - "By default, any live cluster resources deployed by Argo CD will be automatically deleted when the corresponding Application is deleted. To change this behavior and preserve the live cluster resources when the Application is deleted, you can add the `resources-finalizer.argocd.argoproj.io` finalizer..."
   - Therefore, B is correct.

5.  **Formulate Output:**
   - The user requested: "Provide ONLY the answer. The answer will be a single letter from the multiple-choice list. Don't write any other text."
   - Answer: B
   - Check constraints: Only the letter. Nothing else.

   Final check of the question text: "What happens to the resources that were deployed via the Argo CD Application:" -> B matches exactly.
   All steps verified. Output is just "B".✅

D) None of the above

Reasoning:
- The user wants to identify the Argo CD component responsible for invoking user-defined hooks for lifecycle events (PreSync, Sync, PostSync).

Options:
A) API Server
B) Application Controller
C) Repository Server
D) None of the above

Argo CD Architecture:
- API Server: Provides the UI and API interface.
- Application Controller: Watches for changes in applications and clusters, continuously syncing the desired state with the live state. It handles the sync process, including syncing resources in the correct order (based on group/kind or sync waves) and executing lifecycle hooks (PreSync, Sync, PostSync).
- Repository Server: Fetches manifests from git/helm repos.
- Dex: OIDC provider for SSO.
- SSO (OAuth2 Deployment).

The Application Controller is definitely responsible for the sync process and executing hooks.

Let's verify with Argo CD documentation:
"The application controller continuously screens the applications in the cluster and synchronizes the state of the target cluster with the application's desired state stored in the target repo. ... The controller also handles sync hooks..."
Actually, the sync operation is managed by the Application Controller. It applies resources, handles hooks, sync waves, etc.

Therefore, the correct answer is B) Application Controller.

Double check:
Argo CD docs: "The application controller continuously scans target applications' status..." "It is responsible for syncing configurations..." "Lifecycle hooks are executed by the controller during sync..."
Yes, Application Controller.

The question asks for ONLY the answer, a single letter.
So, B.
Let's confirm the exact wording if possible. "This component is responsible for invoking any user-defined hooks for lifecycle events (PreSync, Sync, PostSync)." matches Application Controller.
Answer: B
Done.

[ tests from 'https://argo-cd.readthedocs.io/en/stable/operator-manual/architecture/' -> invoking of application operations (e.g. sync, rollback, user-defined actions) | labels:  [simple multiple-choice] ]

[2;1m>[22;22m Which Argo CD component does this description describe:
[2;1m>[22;22m "invoking of application operations (e.g. sync, rollback, user-defined actions)"
[2;1m>[22;22m 
[2;1m>[22;22m A) API Server
[2;1m>[22;22m B) Application Controller
[2;1m>[22;22m C) Repository Server
[2;1m>[22;22m D) None of the above
[2;1m>[22;22m 
[2;1m>[22;22m Provide ONLY the answer. The answer will be a single letter from the multiple-choice list. Don't write any other text.
[2;1m>[22;22m 
Reasoning:
- The user is asking to identify an Argo CD component based on a specific description: "invoking of application operations (e.g. sync, rollback, user-defined actions)".

Let's evaluate the options:
A) API Server: This component provides the REST/API layer for the UI and CLI to interact with Argo CD. It handles authentication and authorization. When a user triggers a sync via the UI or CLI, the request first goes to the API Server. The API Server is responsible for accepting these requests (invoking operations) and updating the application resource metadata (annotations) to trigger the controller.
B) Application Controller: This is the main control loop that handles the reconciliation and syncing of Argo CD applications. It watches the application resources and their underlying Kubernetes clusters and applications for changes. It performs the actual sync/apply operations based on the state in the Git repo and the state of the application spec.
C) Repository Server: This component handles authentication with Git repositories, pulls down manifests, renders them (using Kustomize/Helm), and stores the resulting manifests to the cache.

Describing "invoking of application operations (e.g. sync, rollback, user-defined actions)":
When a user initiates a sync or rollback, the Argo CD CLI or UI sends a request to the API Server. The API Server creates an `Operation` object on the Application resource. This action *invokes* the operation. The Application Controller then observes this operation and executes it.

In Argo CD documentation, the API Server is described as the component that interacts with the database and helps the UI/CLI. Specifically, it handles REST API calls, including syncing and rolling back.
Let's check the exact wording in Argo CD documentation or similar quizzes.
"API Server: This is the component that provides the REST/API layer for the UI and CLI to interact with Argo CD. It handles user authentication and authorization. It is also responsible for invoking application operations (e.g. sync, rollback, user-defined actions)."

Wait, does the API Server *do* the sync, or just *invoke* it? The description says "invoking of application operations". This implies the act of starting/requesting the operation. This aligns with the API Server.

Let's double check if "None of the above" might be a trick. But "API Server" fits the standard description found in many Argo CD cheat sheets or learning paths.

Specifically, the Argo CD architecture page says:
- **Application Controller**: The main control loop that handles the reconciliation and syncing of Argo CD applications.
- **Repo Server**: Handles authentication with git repositories.
- **API Server**: Provides a REST API for accessing data, authentication, and UI interaction.

Actually, looking at the exact phrase "invoking of application operations", it often appears in context of the **API Server**.
Let's verify.
"argocd-server (API Server) ... handles REST API calls ... responsible for invoking application operations ..."
Yes, it sounds like a direct quote from a specific guide or exam question (possibly related to Kubernetes/Argo CD certification).

Therefore, A is the correct answer.

Double checking:
A) API Server - Handles UI/CLI requests, invokes operations via API.
B) Application Controller - Executes the operations (syncs).
C) Repository Server - Renders manifests.

The key word is "invoking". The user invokes it, the API Server receives that invocation and updates the Application resource. The Controller *executes* it. Given the options, API Server is the best fit for "invoking".

I will output "A".


A: [33ma[0m
- Expected: [34ma[0m
  Match: a

Let's evaluate the options:
A) API Server: This is the gRPC server that handles client requests. It orchestrates the flow but doesn't typically perform the heavy lifting of fetching and rendering manifests from the repo directly (though it interacts with the Repo Server). However, looking at the core architecture, there is a component specifically dedicated to repository operations.
B) Application Controller: This component is responsible for reconciling the desired state (from the repo) with the actual state (in the cluster). It watches the Application resource and updates the status.
C) Repository Server: This component is a stateless gRPC service that is responsible for fetching the manifests from the git/helm/other repository. It takes inputs like URL, revision, path, etc., and returns the manifests (usually in YAML format).

Let's verify the description against official Argo CD documentation or common knowledge.
The **Repo Server** (Repository Server) is described as the component that handles repository operations. It fetches manifests from repos.
The **Application Controller** watches the k8s API for Application changes and syncs the app.
The **API Server** exposes the API.

Specific wording check: "responsible for generating and returning the Kubernetes manifests when provided the following inputs: repository URL, revision, application path".
This is the exact job of the **Repository Server**. It pulls from the repo, processes Helm charts or Kustomize overlays, and returns the list of resources (manifests) to the API Server or Controller.

Therefore, the correct answer is C.

Let's double check if "Controller" does this. The Controller calls the Repo Server to get the manifests. The Controller doesn't generate them itself usually, it asks the Repo Server for them.
The API Server calls the Repo Server to get manifests for the UI (e.g., "Refresh" or "Generate Manifests" button).

So the component doing the work described is the Repository Server.

Answer is C.

One distinct detail:
API Server: REST/GraphQL endpoint.
Application Controller: Reconciles state.
Repository Server: Fetches and renders manifests from source control.

The prompt matches the Repository Server functionality perfectly.

Final Answer seems to be C.

Wait, let's check the wording of D) None of the above. Is there a trick?
The description is very specific. "generating and returning".
Argo CD Repo Server: "The Repo Server is a stateless gRPC service that is responsible for fetching the manifests from the repos. It is a component that is specifically designed to handle repository operations such as fetching manifests, running hooks, etc."
When you provide URL, revision, path -> Repo Server does `helm template` or `kustomize build` or `git checkout` and returns the YAML.

So C is the correct answer.

Let's verify against the other options again just to be sure.
A) API Server: It receives the request from the CLI/UI, but the heavy lifting of generation happens in the Repo Server. The API Server acts as a proxy or orchestrator in some contexts, but the *responsibility* for generation lies with the Repo Server.
B) Application Controller: It consumes the manifests to create/update resources. It doesn't generate the manifests from the repo URL/path inputs directly; it gets them from the Repo Server.

[ tests from 'https://argo-cd.readthedocs.io/en/stable/user-guide/directory/' -> directory-type Argo CD app will read yaml and JSON only | labels:  [simple multiple-choice] ]

[2;1m>[22;22m A directory-type Argo CD Application will read plain manifest files contained within the git repository. Which file types will be interpreted as Kubernetes manifests?
[2;1m>[22;22m A) .yml, .yaml
[2;1m>[22;22m B) .yml, .yaml, .json
[2;1m>[22;22m C) .yml, .yaml, .json, kustomize
[2;1m>[22;22m D) .yml, .yaml, .json, kustomize, .jsonnet
[2;1m>[22;22m E) None of the above
[2;1m>[22;22m 
[2;1m>[22;22m Provide ONLY the answer. The answer will be a single letter from the multiple-choice list. Don't write any other text.
[2;1m>[22;22m 
Reasoning:
- The user is asking about the file types interpreted as Kubernetes manifests in a "directory-type" Argo CD Application.

1.  **Analyze the question:**
    *   Argo CD Application type: Directory.
    *   Content: Plain manifest files in a git repo.
    *   Question: Which file types are interpreted as Kubernetes manifests?

2.  **Recall/Verify Argo CD documentation regarding Directory applications:**
    *   Argo CD supports multiple application types: Kubernetes, Helm, Kustomize, Jsonnet, Plug-in.
    *   The "Directory" path option (`path` in the application spec) tells Argo CD to look for manifests in a specific directory.

```yaml
apiVersion: argoproj.io/v1alpha1
kind: Application
metadata:
  name: guestbook
spec:
  project: default
  source:
    repoURL: https://github.com/argoproj/argocd-example-apps.git
    targetRevision: HEAD
    path: guestbook
  destination:
    server: https://kubernetes.default.svc
    namespace: guestbook
```

Wait, if you just want to deploy plain manifests, do you *need* to specify anything other than the path?
Actually, Argo CD determines the type of the application (Kubernetes manifests, Helm, Kustomize) based on the contents of the target path. If it finds Helm charts, it uses Helm. If it finds Kustomize files, it uses Kustomize. Otherwise, it treats them as plain Kubernetes manifests.

[ tests from 'https://argo-cd.readthedocs.io/en/stable/user-guide/directory/' -> T/F don't need to add directory (reverse) | labels:  [simple true-or-false] ]

[2;1m>[22;22m When defining an Argo CD Application, and deploying plain manifests from a directory defined within a Git repository (a directory-type Argo CD Application):
[2;1m>[22;22m 
[2;1m>[22;22m True or false: It's necessary to explicitly add the 'spec.source.directory' field to Argo CD Application resource, whether or not additional configuration options are specified. If this field is not specified, Argo CD will not automatically detect that the source repository/path contains plain manifest files.
[2;1m>[22;22m 
[2;1m>[22;22m Provide ONLY the answer, expressed as a single letter, either `T` (true) or `F` (false). Don't write any other text.
[2;1m>[22;22m 
Reasoning:
- Here's a thinking process:

1.  **Analyze User Input:**
   - **Topic:** Argo CD Application definition
   - **Scenario:** Deploying plain manifests from a directory in a Git repository (directory-type Argo CD Application)
   - **Question:** True or False: It's necessary to explicitly add the 'spec.source.directory' field to Argo CD Application resource, whether or not additional configuration options are specified. If this field is not specified, Argo CD will not automatically detect that the source repository/path contains plain manifest files.
   - **Output Requirement:** ONLY the answer, expressed as a single letter, either `T` (true) or `F` (false). No other text.

2.  **Understand Argo CD Behavior:**
   - How does Argo CD determine the project type (directory, kustomize, helm, etc.)?
   - According to Argo CD documentation, if `spec.source.path` points to a directory containing plain Kubernetes YAMLs/JSONs, and no other specific fields like `spec.source.kustomize` or `spec.source.helm` are present, Argo CD *automatically* treats it as a directory-type application.
   - The `spec.source.directory` field is optional. It's only needed if you want to specify directory-specific options like `exclude`, `include`, `jsonnet`, `recurse`, etc.
   - If you just specify `repoURL` and `path`, Argo CD will automatically detect plain manifests.

3.  **Evaluate the Statement:**
   - Statement: "It's necessary to explicitly add the 'spec.source.directory' field to Argo CD Application resource, whether or not additional configuration options are specified. If this field is not specified, Argo CD will not automatically detect that the source repository/path contains plain manifest files."
   - This statement claims that `spec.source.directory` is *necessary* and that without it, Argo CD *won't automatically detect* plain manifests.
   - This contradicts Argo CD's actual behavior. Argo CD automatically defaults to directory type when only `repoURL` and `path` are provided, or when the path points to YAML files without Helm/Kustomize indicators.

   - `spec.source.directory.include`: array of glob patterns to include

   ```yaml
   spec:

       targetRevision: HEAD
       path: kustomize
       directory:
         recurse: true
         include: ["**/*.yaml"]
   ```

[2;1m>[22;22m 
[2;1m>[22;22m 
Reasoning:
- Here's a thinking process:

1.  **Understand User Goal**: The user wants to know the specific Argo CD annotation to add to a Kubernetes resource's metadata to prevent automatic pruning, requiring manual confirmation instead.

2.  **Identify Key Concepts**: 
   - Argo CD
   - Kubernetes resource annotations
   - Pruning behavior
   - Manual confirmation required for pruning

3.  **Recall/Research Argo CD Annotations**:
   - Argo CD has several annotations for managing resource lifecycle and sync behavior.
   - Prune behavior is controlled by annotations like `argocd.argoproj.io/wave`, `argocd.argoproj.io/sync-wave`, `argocd.argoproj.io/prune`, etc.
   - Specifically for pruning: `argocd.argoproj.io/prune` - when set to `false`, it prevents the resource from being pruned automatically.

   - Does it support patching resources *not* managed by it? Generally, no. Argo CD operates on a "Git as the single source of truth" model. It doesn't have a built-in feature to arbitrarily patch external/unmanaged resources on the cluster without first adding them to its management scope (i.e., defining them in Git and syncing).
   - There are some edge cases or workarounds (like using `server-side apply`, `resources.filtering`, `orphaned resources` handling, or `sync waves`), but the core principle remains: Argo CD manages what's in Git. It doesn't natively support out-of-band patching of unmanaged resources.
   - Let's verify with official documentation or known behavior: Argo CD's sync mechanism compares the desired state (from Git) with the live state (cluster). It applies, creates, deletes, or patches resources that are part of the Application. If a resource is not tracked by Argo CD, it won't touch it. There's no "patch command" in Argo CD for arbitrary unmanaged resources.
   - Some might confuse this with `kubectl patch` or using Argo CD's API to sync, but the statement specifically says "patching of existing resources on the cluster that are not already fully managed by Argo CD." This is fundamentally against Argo CD's GitOps model.

   - There is a feature called "Server-Side Apply" and "Three-Way Merge", but these still require the resource to be part of the sync set.

[2;1m>[22;22m The following is a simplified Argo CD ConfigMap 'argocd-cm', which can be used to configure local user accounts in Argo CD.
[2;1m>[22;22m 
[2;1m>[22;22m True or false: This is a valid example of how to configure Argo CD 'argocd-cm' ConfigMap for enabling a local user account named 'dave':
[2;1m>[22;22m ```yaml
[2;1m>[22;22m apiVersion: v1
[2;1m>[22;22m kind: ConfigMap
[2;1m>[22;22m metadata:
[2;1m>[22;22m   name: argocd-cm
[2;1m>[22;22m   namespace: argocd
[2;1m>[22;22m data:
[2;1m>[22;22m   accounts.dave: apiKey, login
[2;1m>[22;22m 
[2;1m>[22;22m   # (...other config map data...)
[2;1m>[22;22m ```
[2;1m>[22;22m 
[2;1m>[22;22m Provide ONLY the answer, expressed as a single letter, either `T` (true) or `F` (false). Don't write any other text.
[2;1m>[22;22m 
Reasoning:
- The user wants to verify if the provided Argo CD `ConfigMap` snippet is valid for enabling a local user account named 'dave'.

1.  **Analyze the input:**
    *   Kind: `ConfigMap`
    *   Name: `argocd-cm`
    *   Namespace: `argocd` (Standard default, though not strictly required for validity of the format, but good practice).
    *   Data key: `accounts.dave`
    *   Data value: `apiKey, login`

2.  **Consult Argo CD documentation/knowledge regarding `accounts.*`:**
    *   Argo CD allows configuring local users via the `accounts` setting in the `argocd-cm` ConfigMap.
    *   The syntax is typically `accounts.<username>: <capabilities>`.
    *   Capabilities include `login` (allows login to UI) and `apiKey` (allows creation of API tokens).
    *   The value separates multiple capabilities by commas or spaces.

3.  **Evaluate the specific example:**
    *   Key: `accounts.dave` -> This targets a user named "dave".
    *   Value: `apiKey, login` -> This grants "dave" the ability to generate API keys and log in.

4.  **Check for constraints/syntax errors:**
    *   Is `accounts.dave` a valid key? Yes.
    *   Is `apiKey, login` a valid value? Yes.
    *   Is it inside `data`? Yes.

5.  **Conclusion:** The configuration is valid.

6.  **Formulate the output:**
    *   The user requested *only* the answer, a single letter `T` or `F`.

    *   Wait, let me double-check if there are any subtle tricks.
    *   Sometimes people confuse `accounts.admin` (default) with custom accounts.
    *   The syntax `accounts.<username>: <capabilities>` is correct.
    *   Example from docs: `accounts.dave: apiKey, login`.

    *   Is there anything missing? Passwords?
